<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Anarchism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>

</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Mikhail Alexandrovich Bakunin</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/uː/ long 'oo' in 'food'">uː</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">Михаил Александрович Бакунин</span>; <small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Russian" title="Help:IPA for Russian">[mʲɪxɐˈil ɐlʲɪkʲsɐnʲtrəˈfit͡ɕ bɐˈkunʲɪn]</a></span>; 30 May [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 18 May] 1814 – 1 July 1876) was a Russian <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary" title="Revolutionary">revolutionary</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist" title="Anarchist">anarchist</a>, and founder of <a href="/wiki/Collectivist_anarchism" title="Collectivist anarchism">collectivist anarchism</a>. He is considered among the most influential figures of <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a>, and one of the principal founders of the <a href="/wiki/Social_anarchism" title="Social anarchism">social anarchist</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Bakunin's enormous prestige as an activist made him one of the most famous ideologues in Europe, and he gained substantial influence among radicals throughout Russia and Europe.</p>
<p>Bakunin grew up in <a href="/wiki/Kuvshinovsky_District" title="Kuvshinovsky District">Pryamukhino</a>, a family estate in <a href="/wiki/Tver_Governorate" title="Tver Governorate">Tver Governorate</a>, where he moved to study philosophy and began to read the French <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A9distes" title="Encyclopédistes">encyclopédistes</a>, leading to enthusiasm for the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte" title="Johann Gottlieb Fichte">Fichte</a>. From Fichte, Bakunin went on to immerse himself in the works of <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Hegel</a>, the most influential thinker among German intellectuals at the time. That led to his embrace of <a href="/wiki/Hegelianism" title="Hegelianism">Hegelianism</a>, bedazzled by Hegel's famous maxim, "Everything that exists is rational." In 1840, Bakunin traveled to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Petersburg" title="St. Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a> and Berlin with the intention of preparing himself for a professorship in philosophy or history at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Moscow" title="University of Moscow">University of Moscow</a>. In 1842, Bakunin moved from Berlin to Dresden. Eventually he arrived in Paris, where he met <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>.</p>
<p>Bakunin's increasing radicalism – including staunch opposition to imperialism in east and central Europe by Russia and other powers – changed his life, putting an end to hopes of a professorial career. He was eventually deported from France for speaking against Russia's oppression of Poland. In 1849, Bakunin was apprehended in Dresden for his participation in the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">Czech rebellion of 1848</a>, and turned over to Russia where he was imprisoned in the <a href="/wiki/Peter_and_Paul_Fortress" title="Peter and Paul Fortress">Peter-Paul Fortress</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>. He remained there until 1857, when he was exiled to a work camp in Siberia. Escaping to Japan, the US and finally ending up in London for a short time, he worked with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Herzen" title="Alexander Herzen">Alexander Herzen</a> on the journal <i>Kolokol</i> (<i>The Bell</i>). In 1863, he left to join the <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">insurrection in Poland</a>, but he failed to reach his destination and instead spent some time in Switzerland and Italy.</p>
<p>In 1868, Bakunin joined the socialist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Working_Men%27s_Association" title="International Working Men's Association">International Working Men's Association</a>, a federation of trade unions and workers' organizations, which had sections in many European countries, as well as in Latin America and (after 1872) in North Africa and the Middle East. The "Bakuninist" or anarchist trend rapidly expanded in influence, especially in Spain, which constituted the largest section of the International at the time. A showdown loomed with Marx, who was a key figure in the General Council of the International. The 1872 <a href="/wiki/Hague_Congress_(1872)" title="Hague Congress (1872)">Hague Congress</a> was dominated by a struggle between Marx and his followers, who argued for the use of the state to bring about socialism, and the Bakunin/anarchist faction, which argued instead for the replacement of the state by federations of self-governing workplaces and communes. Bakunin could not attend the congress, as he could not reach the Netherlands. Bakunin's faction present at the conference lost, and Bakunin was (in Marx's view) expelled for supposedly maintaining a secret organisation within the international.</p>
<p>However, the anarchists insisted the congress was unrepresentative and exceeded its powers, and held a rival conference of the International at <a href="/wiki/Saint-Imier" title="Saint-Imier">Saint-Imier</a> in Switzerland in 1872. This repudiated the Hague meeting, including Bakunin's supposed expulsion. The great majority of sections of the International affiliated to the St. Imier body, making Marx's victory rather more illusory than pro-Marxist accounts suggest. The far larger Bakuninist international outlasted its small Marxist rival, which was isolated in New York; it also greatly facilitated the global spread of anarcho-socialism. In the International, as well as in his writings, Bakunin articulated the basic ideas of <a href="/wiki/Syndicalism" title="Syndicalism">syndicalism</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a>, and developed the basic anarchist analysis and strategy. He had by this stage abandoned the anti-imperialist nationalism of his youth.</p>
<p>From 1870 to 1876, Bakunin wrote some of his longer works, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Statism_and_Anarchy" title="Statism and Anarchy">Statism and Anarchy</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/God_and_the_State" title="God and the State">God and the State</a></i>. Bakunin remained, however, a direct participant in struggles. In 1870, he was involved in an insurrection in <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a>, France, which foreshadowed the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Commune" title="Paris Commune">Paris Commune</a>. The Paris Commune closely corresponded to many elements of Bakunin's anarchist programme – self-management, mandates delegates, a militia system with elected officers, and decentralisation. Anarchists like <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lis%C3%A9e_Reclus" title="Élisée Reclus">Élisée Reclus</a>, and those in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a> – who had greatly influenced Bakunin – were key figures in the Commune. Despite declining health, much a result of his years of imprisonment, Bakunin also sought to take part in a communal insurrection involving anarchists in <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>, Italy, but was forced to return to Switzerland in disguise, where he settled in <a href="/wiki/Lugano" title="Lugano">Lugano</a>. He remained active in the worker's and peasant's movements of Europe and was also a major influence on movements in Egypt and Latin America.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the spring of 1814, Mikhail Alexandrovich Bakunin "was born to a noble family of only modest means"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sale_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sale-3">[3]</a></sup> – the family owned 500 serfs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> – in the village of <a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%9F%D1%80%D1%8F%D0%BC%D1%83%D1%85%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE" title="ru:Прямухино">Pryamukhino</a> (Прямухино), between <a href="/wiki/Torzhok" title="Torzhok">Torzhok</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuvshinovo" title="Kuvshinovo">Kuvshinovo</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Tver_Governorate" title="Tver Governorate">Tver Governorate</a>, northwest of Moscow. His father was a career diplomat who, as a young attache, had lived for years in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>. Upon his return to Russia, his father settled down on his paternal estate where at the age of forty, he married an eighteen-year-old girl from the prominent Muravyov family. His father's commitment to liberal ideas led to his involvement with one of the <a href="/wiki/Decembrist_revolt" title="Decembrist revolt">Decembrist</a> clubs. After <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia" title="Nicholas I of Russia">Nicolas I</a> became Tsar, however, Bakunin senior gave up politics and devoted himself to the care of his estate and the education of his children, five girls and five boys, the oldest of whom was Mikhail (Michael).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the age of 14 Michael left for <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, receiving military training at the Artillery University, "a rigid, anti-Western military school, where he chafed at the arbitrary discipline and the narrow curriculum—much less encompassing than the homeschooling he had experienced before."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sale_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sale-3">[3]</a></sup> He was "expelled from school in 1834 for poor grades and assigned to barracks on the Polish frontier."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sale_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sale-3">[3]</a></sup> He was commissioned a junior officer in the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Guard_(Russia)" title="Imperial Guard (Russia)">Russian Imperial Guard</a> and sent to <a href="/wiki/Minsk" title="Minsk">Minsk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grodno" title="Grodno">Gardinas</a> in Lithuania (now <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>). After two years he left the Imperial Guard, as he could not agree on how the <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Polish people</a> were treated by the Russian authorities. Since that time, the fate of the Polish national liberation struggle became Bakunin's constant interest. Though his father wished him to continue in either the military or the civil service, Bakunin abandoned both in 1835, and made his way to Moscow, hoping to study philosophy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interest_in_philosophy">Interest in philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Interest in philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Moscow, Bakunin soon became friends with a group of former university students, and engaged in the systematic study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idealist" title="Idealist">Idealist</a> philosophy, grouped around the poet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolay_Stankevich" title="Nikolay Stankevich">Nikolay Stankevich</a>, "the bold pioneer who opened to Russian thought the vast and fertile continent of German metaphysics" (<a href="/wiki/E._H._Carr" title="E. H. Carr">E. H. Carr</a>). The philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a> initially was central to their study, but they progressed to <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Joseph_Schelling" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Joseph Schelling">Schelling</a>, Fichte, and Hegel. By autumn 1835, Bakunin had conceived of forming a philosophical circle in his home town of Pryamukhino. Moreover, by early 1836, Bakunin was back in Moscow, where he published translations of Fichte's <i>Some Lectures Concerning the Scholar's Vocation</i> and <i>The Way to a Blessed Life</i>, which became his favorite book. With Stankevich he also read <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goethe" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schiller" title="Schiller">Schiller</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E.T.A._Hoffmann" title="E.T.A. Hoffmann">E.T.A. Hoffmann</a>.</p>
<p>He became increasingly influenced by Hegel and provided the first Russian translation of his work. During this period he met <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavophile" title="Slavophile">slavophile</a> <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Aksakov" title="Konstantin Aksakov">Konstantin Aksakov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Chaadayev" title="Pyotr Chaadayev">Piotr Chaadaev</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialists</a> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Herzen" title="Alexander Herzen">Alexander Herzen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikolay_Ogarev" title="Nikolay Ogarev">Nikolay Ogarev</a>. In this period he began to develop his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan-slavism" title="Pan-slavism">panslavic</a> views. After long wrangles with his father, Bakunin went to Berlin in 1840. His stated plan at the time was still to become a university professor (a “priest of truth,” as he and his friends imagined it), but he soon encountered and joined students of the <a href="/wiki/Young_Hegelians" title="Young Hegelians">Young Hegelians</a> and the socialist movement in Berlin. In his 1842 essay "The Reaction in Germany", he argued in favor of the revolutionary role of negation, summed up in the phrase "the passion for destruction is a creative passion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>After three semesters in Berlin, Bakunin went to <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a> where he became friends with <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Ruge" title="Arnold Ruge">Arnold Ruge</a>. Here he also read <a href="/wiki/Lorenz_von_Stein" title="Lorenz von Stein">Lorenz von Stein</a>'s <i>Der Sozialismus und Kommunismus des heutigen Frankreich</i> and developed a passion for socialism. He abandoned his interest in an academic career, devoting more and more of his time to promoting revolution. The Russian government, becoming aware of this activity, ordered him to return to Russia. On his refusal his property was confiscated. Instead he went with <a href="/wiki/Georg_Herwegh" title="Georg Herwegh">Georg Herwegh</a> to Zürich, Switzerland.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland.2C_Brussels.2C_Prague.2C_Dresden_and_Paris">Switzerland, Brussels, Prague, Dresden and Paris</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Switzerland, Brussels, Prague, Dresden and Paris">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Bakunin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="302" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Young_Bakunin.jpg/220px-Young_Bakunin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/32/Young_Bakunin.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Bakunin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The young Mikhail Bakunin, illustrated in 1843</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During his six-month stay in Zürich, he became closely associated with German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communist" title="Communist">communist</a> <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Weitling" title="Wilhelm Weitling">Wilhelm Weitling</a>. Until 1848 he remained on friendly terms with the German communists, occasionally calling himself a communist and writing articles on communism in the <i>Schweitzerische Republikaner</i>. He moved to Geneva in western Switzerland shortly before Weitling's arrest. His name had appeared frequently in Weitling's correspondence seized by the police. This led to reports being circulated to the imperial police. The Russian ambassador in Bern ordered Bakunin to return to Russia, but instead he went to <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, where he met many leading Polish nationalists, such as <a href="/wiki/Joachim_Lelewel" title="Joachim Lelewel">Joachim Lelewel</a>, co-member with <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a> at Brussels. Lelewel greatly influenced him; however, he clashed with the Polish nationalists over their demand for a historic Poland based on the borders of 1776 (before the <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a>) as he defended the right of autonomy for the non-Polish peoples in these territories. He also did not support their <a href="/wiki/Clericalism" title="Clericalism">clericalism</a> and they did not support his calls for the emancipation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peasantry" title="Peasantry">peasantry</a>.</p>
<p>In 1844 Bakunin went to Paris, then a centre of the European political current. He established contact with <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a>, who greatly impressed him and with whom he formed a personal bond. In December 1844, Emperor Nicholas issued a decree that stripped Bakunin of his privileges as a <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">noble</a>, confiscated his land in Russia, and condemned him to lifelong exile in Siberia. He responded with a long letter to <i>La Réforme</i>, denouncing the Emperor as a despot and calling for democracy in Russia and Poland (Carr, p. 139). In March 1846 in another letter to the <i>Constitutionel</i> he defended Poland, following the repression of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" title="Roman Catholicism">Catholics</a> there. Some Polish refugees from <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>, following the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w_Uprising" title="Kraków Uprising">uprising</a> there, invited him to speak<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> at the meeting in November 1847 commemorating the Polish <a href="/wiki/November_Uprising" title="November Uprising">November Uprising</a> of 1830.</p>
<p>In his speech, Bakunin called for an alliance between the Polish and Russian peoples against the Emperor, and looked forward to "the definitive collapse of despotism in Russia." As a result, he was expelled from France and went to Brussels. Bakunin's attempt to draw <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Herzen" title="Alexander Herzen">Alexander Herzen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vissarion_Belinsky" title="Vissarion Belinsky">Vissarion Belinsky</a> into conspiratorial action for revolution in Russia fell on deaf ears. In Brussels, Bakunin renewed his contacts with revolutionary Poles and Karl Marx. He spoke at a meeting organised by Lelewel in February 1848 about a great future for the Slavs, whose destiny was to rejuvenate the Western world. Around this time the Russian embassy circulated rumours that Bakunin was a Russian agent who had exceeded his orders.</p>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">revolutionary movement of 1848</a> broke out, Bakunin was ecstatic, despite disappointment that little was happening in Russia. Bakunin obtained funding from some socialists in the Provisional Government, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Flocon" title="Ferdinand Flocon">Ferdinand Flocon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Blanc" title="Louis Blanc">Louis Blanc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Auguste_Ledru-Rollin" title="Alexandre Auguste Ledru-Rollin">Alexandre Auguste Ledru-Rollin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Martin" title="Alexandre Martin">Alexandre Martin</a>, for a project for a Slav federation liberating those under the rule of Prussia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austro-Hungary" title="Austro-Hungary">Austro-Hungary</a> and Ottoman Empire. He left for Germany travelling through <a href="/wiki/Baden" title="Baden">Baden</a> to <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ln" title="Köln">Köln</a>.</p>
<p>Bakunin supported the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=German_Democratic_Legion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="German Democratic Legion (page does not exist)">German Democratic Legion</a> led by Herwegh in an abortive attempt to join <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hecker" title="Friedrich Hecker">Friedrich Hecker</a>'s insurrection in <a href="/wiki/Baden" title="Baden">Baden</a>. He broke with Marx over the latter's criticism of Herwegh. Much later in 1871 Bakunin was to write:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"I must openly admit that in this controversy Marx and Engels were in the right. With characteristic insolence, they attacked Herwegh personally when he was not there to defend himself. In a face-to-face confrontation with them, I heatedly defended Herwegh, and our mutual dislike began then."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Bakunin went on to Berlin but was stopped by the police from traveling to <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Posen</a>, part of Polish territories gained by Prussia in the <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a>, where a nationalist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wielkopolska_Uprising_(1848)" title="Wielkopolska Uprising (1848)">insurrection</a> was taking place. Instead Bakunin went to <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breslau" title="Breslau">Breslau</a> and then on to <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> where he participated in the First Pan Slav Congress. The Congress was followed by an <a href="/wiki/May_Conspiracy" title="May Conspiracy">abortive insurrection</a> that Bakunin had sought to promote and intensify but which was violently suppressed.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a> writes in his autobiography about Bakunin's visit:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>First of all, however, with the view of adapting himself to the most Philistine culture, he had to submit his huge beard and bushy hair to the tender mercies of the razor and shears. As no barber was available, Rockel had to undertake the task. A small group of friends watched the operation, which had to be executed with a dull razor, causing no little pain, under which none but the victim himself remained passive. We bade farewell to Bakunin with the firm conviction that we should never see him again alive. But in a week he was back once more, as he had realised immediately what a distorted account he had received as to the state of things in Prague, where all he found ready for him was a mere handful of childish students. These admissions made him the butt of Rockel's good-humoured chaff, and after this he won the reputation among us of being a mere revolutionary, who was content with theoretical conspiracy. Very similar to his expectations from the Prague students were his presumptions with regard to the Russian people.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>He returned to Breslau, where Marx republished the allegation that Bakunin was an imperial agent, claiming that <a href="/wiki/George_Sand" title="George Sand">George Sand</a> had proof. Marx retracted the statement after George Sand came to Bakunin's defense<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p>Bakunin published his <i>Appeal to the Slavs</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> in the fall of 1848, in which he proposed that Slav revolutionaries unite with Hungarian, Italian and German revolutionaries to overthrow the three major European autocracies, the Russian Empire, the Austro-Hungarian Empire, and the Kingdom of Prussia.</p>
<p>Bakunin played a leading role in the <a href="/wiki/May_Uprising_in_Dresden" title="May Uprising in Dresden">May Uprising in Dresden</a> in 1849, helping to organize the defense of the barricades against Prussian troops with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Heine" title="Wilhelm Heine">Wilhelm Heine</a>. Bakunin was captured in <a href="/wiki/Chemnitz" title="Chemnitz">Chemnitz</a> and held for thirteen months before being condemned to death by the government of <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxony</a>. His sentence was commuted to life to allow his extradition to Russia and Austria both of whom were seeking to prosecute him. In June 1850, he was handed over to the Austrian authorities. Eleven months later he received a further death sentence but this too was commuted to life imprisonment. Finally, in May 1851, Bakunin was handed over to the Russian authorities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imprisonment.2C_.22confession.22.2C_and_exile">Imprisonment, "confession", and exile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title='Edit section: Imprisonment, "confession", and exile'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bakunin was taken to the <a href="/wiki/Peter_and_Paul_Fortress" title="Peter and Paul Fortress">Peter and Paul Fortress</a>. At the beginning of his captivity, Count <a href="/wiki/Orlov_(family)" title="Orlov (family)">Orlov</a>, an emissary of the Tsar, visited Bakunin and told him that the Tsar requested a written confession<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> hoping that the confession would place Bakunin spiritually as well as physically in the power of the Russian state. Since all his acts were known, he had no secrets to reveal, and so he decided to write to the Tsar, "You want my confession; but you must know that a penitent sinner is not obliged to implicate or reveal the misdeeds of others. I have only the honor and the conscience that I have never betrayed anyone who has confided in me, and this is why I will not give you any names."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>After three years in the underground dungeons of the Fortress of St Peter and St Paul, he spent another four years in the castle of <a href="/wiki/Shlisselburg" title="Shlisselburg">Shlisselburg</a>. It was here that he suffered from <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a> and all his teeth fell out as a result of the diet. He later recounted that he found some relief in mentally re-enacting the legend of <a href="/wiki/Prometheus" title="Prometheus">Prometheus</a>. His continuing imprisonment in these awful conditions led him to entreat his brother to supply him with poison.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mikhail_Bakunin_and_Antonia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="868" data-file-width="597" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mikhail_Bakunin_and_Antonia.jpg/170px-Mikhail_Bakunin_and_Antonia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mikhail_Bakunin_and_Antonia.jpg/255px-Mikhail_Bakunin_and_Antonia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mikhail_Bakunin_and_Antonia.jpg/340px-Mikhail_Bakunin_and_Antonia.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mikhail_Bakunin_and_Antonia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mikhail Bakunin and Antonia Kwiatkowska, circa 1861</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Novelist <a href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Solzhenitsyn" title="Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn">Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn</a> in his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gulag_Archipelago" title="The Gulag Archipelago">The Gulag Archipelago</a></i> (published in 1973) recounts that Bakunin "...abjectly groveled before Nicholas I - thereby avoiding execution. Was this wretchedness of soul? Or revolutionary cunning?" <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the death of Nicholas I, the new Tsar, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Russia" title="Alexander II of Russia">Alexander II</a> personally struck Bakunin's name off the amnesty list. In February 1857 his mother's pleas to the Tsar were finally heeded and he was allowed to go into permanent exile in the western Siberian city of <a href="/wiki/Tomsk" title="Tomsk">Tomsk</a>. Within a year of arriving in Tomsk, Bakunin married Antonia Kwiatkowska, the daughter of a Polish merchant. He had been teaching her French. In August 1858 Bakunin received a visit from his second cousin, General Count <a href="/wiki/Nikolay_Muravyov-Amursky" title="Nikolay Muravyov-Amursky">Nikolay Muravyov-Amursky</a>, who had been governor of Eastern Siberia for ten years.</p>
<p>Muravyov was a liberal and Bakunin, as his relative, became a particular favourite. In the spring of 1859 Muravyov helped Bakunin with a job for Amur Development Agency which enabled him to move with his wife to <a href="/wiki/Irkutsk" title="Irkutsk">Irkutsk</a>, the capital of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Siberia" title="Eastern Siberia">Eastern Siberia</a>. This enabled Bakunin to be part of the circle involved in political discussions centred on Muravyov's colonial headquarters. Resenting the treatment of the colony by the Saint Petersburg bureaucracy, including its use as a dumping ground for malcontents, a proposal for a <i>United States of Siberia</i> emerged, independent of Russia and federated into a new <i>United States of Siberia and America</i>, following the example of the United States of America. The circle included Muravyov's young Chief of Staff, Kukel – who <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Kropotkin</a> related had the complete works of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Herzen" title="Alexander Herzen">Alexander Herzen</a> – the civil governor Izvolsky, who allowed Bakunin to use his address for correspondence, and Muravyov's deputy and eventual successor, General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Dondukov-Korsakov" title="Alexander Dondukov-Korsakov">Alexander Dondukov-Korsakov</a>.</p>
<p>When Herzen criticised Muravyov in <i>The Bell</i>, Bakunin wrote vigorously in his patron's defence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Bakunin tired of his job as a commercial traveller, but thanks to Muravyov's influence, was able to keep his sinecure (worth 2,000 roubles a year) without having to perform any duties. Muravyov was forced to retire from his post as governor general, partly because of his liberal views and partly due to fears he might take Siberia towards independence. He was replaced by Korsakov, who perhaps was even more sympathetic to the plight of the Siberian exiles. Korsakov was also related to Bakunin, Bakunin's brother Paul having married his cousin. Taking Bakunin's word, Korsakov issued him with a letter giving him passage on all ships on the <a href="/wiki/Amur_River" title="Amur River">Amur River</a> and its tributaries as long as he was back in Irkutsk when the ice came.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Escape_from_exile_and_return_to_Europe">Escape from exile and return to Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Escape from exile and return to Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 5 June 1861 Bakunin left <a href="/wiki/Irkutsk" title="Irkutsk">Irkutsk</a> under cover of company business, ostensibly employed by a Siberian merchant to make a trip to <a href="/wiki/Nikolayevsk-on-Amur" title="Nikolayevsk-on-Amur">Nikolaevsk</a>. By 17 July he was on board the Russian warship <i>Strelok</i> bound for Kastri. However, in the port of <a href="/wiki/Olga,_Russia" title="Olga, Russia">Olga</a>, Bakunin managed to persuade the American captain of the SS <i>Vickery</i> to take him on board. Despite bumping into the Russian Consul on board, Bakunin was able to sail away under the nose of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Imperial_Navy" title="Russian Imperial Navy">Russian Imperial Navy</a>. By 6 August he had reached <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hakodate" title="Hakodate">Hakodate</a> in the northernmost Japanese island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hokkaid%C5%8D" title="Hokkaidō">Hokkaidō</a> and was soon in <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a>.</p>
<p>In Japan Bakunin met by chance <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Heine" title="Wilhelm Heine">Wilhelm Heine</a>, one of his comrades-in arms from Dresden. He also met the German botanist <a href="/wiki/Philipp_Franz_von_Siebold" title="Philipp Franz von Siebold">Philipp Franz von Siebold</a> who had been involved in opening up Japan to Europeans (particularly Russians and the Dutch) and was a friend of Bakunin's patron Muraviev.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Von Siebold's son wrote some 40 years later:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In that Yokohama boarding-house we encountered an outlaw from the Wild West Heine, presumably as well as many other interesting guests. The presence of the Russian revolutionist Michael Bakunin, in flight from Siberia, was as far as one could see being winked at by the authorities. He was well-endowed with money, and none who came to know him could fail to pay their respects.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Bakunin, his ideas still developing, left Japan from <a href="/wiki/Kanagawa-ku,_Yokohama" title="Kanagawa-ku, Yokohama">Kanagawa</a> on the SS <i>Carrington</i>, as one of nineteen passengers including Heine, Rev. P. F. Koe and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Heco" title="Joseph Heco">Joseph Heco</a>. Heco was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_American" title="Japanese American">Japanese American</a>, who eight years later played a significant role giving political advice to <a href="/wiki/Kido_Takayoshi" title="Kido Takayoshi">Kido Takayoshi</a> and <a href="/wiki/It%C5%8D_Hirobumi" title="Itō Hirobumi">Itō Hirobumi</a> during the revolutionary overthrow of the feudal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Late Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> They arrived in San Francisco on 15 October. In the period before the transcontinental railroads had been completed, the quickest way to New York was via Panama. Bakunin boarded the <i>Orizaba</i> for Panama, where after waiting for two weeks he boarded the <i>Champion</i> for New York.</p>
<p>In Boston, Bakunin visited <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Karol_Forster&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Karol Forster (page does not exist)">Karol Forster</a>, a partisan of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ludwik_Mieroslawski" title="Ludwik Mieroslawski">Ludwik Mieroslawski</a> during the 1848 Revolution in Paris, and caught up with other "<a href="/wiki/Forty-Eighters" title="Forty-Eighters">Forty-Eighters</a>", veterans of the 1848 revolutions in Europe, such as <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Kapp" title="Friedrich Kapp">Friedrich Kapp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> He then sailed for <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> arriving on December 27. Bakunin immediately went to London to see Herzen. That evening he burst into the drawing-room where the family was having supper. "What! Are you sitting down eating oysters! Well! Tell me the news. What is happening, and where?!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relocation_to_Italy_and_influence_in_Spain">Relocation to Italy and influence in Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Relocation to Italy and influence in Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Having re-entered Western Europe, Bakunin immediately immersed himself in the revolutionary movement. In 1860, while still in <a href="/wiki/Irkutsk" title="Irkutsk">Irkutsk</a> Bakunin and his political associates had been greatly impressed by <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Garibaldi" title="Giuseppe Garibaldi">Giuseppe Garibaldi</a> and his expedition to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two_Sicilies" title="Two Sicilies">Sicily</a>, during which he declared himself dictator in the name of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Emmanuel_II" title="Victor Emmanuel II">Victor Emmanuel II</a>. Following his return to London, he wrote to Garibaldi on 31 January 1862:</p>
<dl>
<dd>If you could have seen as I did the passionate enthusiasm of the whole town of Irkutsk, the capital of Eastern Siberia, at the news of your triumphal march across the possession of the mad king of Naples, you would have said as I did that there is no longer space or frontiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Bakunin asked Garibaldi to participate in a movement encompassing Italians, Hungarians and <a href="/wiki/South_Slavs" title="South Slavs">South Slavs</a> against both Austria and Turkey. Garibaldi was then engaged in preparations for the <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Garibaldi#Expedition_against_Rome" title="Giuseppe Garibaldi">Expedition against Rome</a>. By May Bakunin's correspondence was focussing on Italian-Slavic unity and the developments in Poland. By June he had resolved to move to Italy, but was waiting for his wife to join him. When he left for Italy in August, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazzini" title="Mazzini">Mazzini</a> wrote to Maurizio Quadrio, one of his key supporters that Bakunin was a good and dependable person. However, with the news of the failure at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aspromonte" title="Battle of Aspromonte">Aspromonte</a> Bakunin paused in Paris where he was briefly involved with <a href="/wiki/Ludwik_Mieros%C5%82awski" title="Ludwik Mierosławski">Ludwik Mierosławski</a>. However Bakunin rejected Mieroslawski's chauvinism and refusal to grant any concessions to the peasants.</p>
<p>Bakunin returned to England in September and focussed on Polish affairs. When the <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">Polish insurrection</a> broke out in January 1863, he sailed to Copenhagen where he hoped to join the Polish insurgents. They planned to sail across the Baltic in the SS <i>Ward Jackson</i> to join the insurrection. This attempt failed, and Bakunin met his wife in Stockholm before returning to London. Now he focussed again on going to Italy and his friend Aurelio Saffi wrote him letters of introduction for <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>. Mazzini wrote letters of commendation to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Federico_Campanella&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Federico Campanella (page does not exist)">Federico Campanella</a> in <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Giuseppe_Dolfi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Giuseppe Dolfi (page does not exist)">Giuseppe Dolfi</a> in Florence. Bakunin left London in November 1863 travelling by way of Brussels, Paris and <a href="/wiki/Vevey" title="Vevey">Vevey</a> (Switzerland) arriving in Italy on 11 January 1864. It was here that he first began to develop his anarchist ideas.</p>
<p>He conceived the plan of forming a secret organization of revolutionaries to carry on propaganda work and prepare for direct action. He recruited Italians, Frenchmen, Scandinavians, and Slavs into the International Brotherhood, also called the Alliance of Revolutionary Socialists.</p>
<p>By July 1866 Bakunin was informing Herzen and Ogarev about the fruits of his work over the previous two years. His secret society then had members in Sweden, Norway, Denmark, Belgium, England, France, Spain, and Italy, as well as Polish and Russian members. In his <i><a href="/wiki/Catechism_of_a_Revolutionary" title="Catechism of a Revolutionary">Catechism of a Revolutionary</a></i> of 1866, he opposed religion and the state, advocating the "absolute rejection of every authority including that which sacrifices freedom for the convenience of the state."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bakunin_Membership_Card.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="350" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Bakunin_Membership_Card.jpg/220px-Bakunin_Membership_Card.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Bakunin_Membership_Card.jpg/330px-Bakunin_Membership_Card.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/Bakunin_Membership_Card.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bakunin_Membership_Card.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bakunin's membership card of the <i>League of Peace and Freedom</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Fanelli" title="Giuseppe Fanelli">Giuseppe Fanelli</a> met Bakunin at <a href="/wiki/Ischia" title="Ischia">Ischia</a> in 1866.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBookchin199814_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBookchin199814-20">[20]</a></sup> In October 1868 Bakunin sponsored Fanelli to travel to <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a> to share his libertarian visions and recruit revolutionists to the <a href="/wiki/International_Workingmen%27s_Association" title="International Workingmen's Association">International Workingmen's Association</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBookchin199812.E2.80.9315_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBookchin199812.E2.80.9315-21">[21]</a></sup> Fanellis trip and the meeting he organised during his travels provided the catalyst for the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Republican_government_in_exile" title="Spanish Republican government in exile">Spanish exiles</a>, the largest workers' and peasants' movement in modern Spain and the largest Anarchist movement in modern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBookchin199812_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBookchin199812-22">[22]</a></sup> Fanelli's tour took him first to Barcelona, where he met and stayed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elis%C3%A9e_Reclus" title="Elisée Reclus">Elisée Reclus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBookchin199812_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBookchin199812-22">[22]</a></sup> Reclus and Fanelli were at odds over Reclus' friendships with Spanish republicans, and Fanelli soon left Barcelona for Madrid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBookchin199812_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBookchin199812-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Fanelli stayed in Madrid until the end of January 1869, conducting meetings to introduce Spanish workers, including <a href="/wiki/Anselmo_Lorenzo" title="Anselmo Lorenzo">Anselmo Lorenzo</a>, to the First International.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBookchin199813_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBookchin199813-24">[24]</a></sup> In February 1869 Fanelli left Madrid, journeying home via Barcelona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBookchin199814_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBookchin199814-20">[20]</a></sup> While in Barcelona again, he met with painter <a href="/wiki/Josep_Llu%C3%ADs_Pellicer" title="Josep Lluís Pellicer">Josep Lluís Pellicer</a> and his cousin, <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Farga_i_Pellicer" title="Rafael Farga i Pellicer">Rafael Farga Pellicer</a> along with others who were to play an important role establishing the International in Barcelona,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBookchin199814_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBookchin199814-20">[20]</a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/International_Alliance_of_Socialist_Democracy" title="International Alliance of Socialist Democracy">Alliance</a> section.</p>
<p>During the 1867–1868 period, Bakunin responded to <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Acollas" title="Émile Acollas">Émile Acollas</a>'s call and became involved in the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Peace_and_Freedom" title="League of Peace and Freedom">League of Peace and Freedom</a> (LPF), for which he wrote a lengthy essay <i>Federalism, Socialism, and Anti-Theologism</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Here he advocated a federalist socialism, drawing on the work of Proudhon. He supported freedom of association and the right of secession for each unit of the federation, but emphasized that this freedom must be joined with socialism for: "Liberty without socialism is privilege, injustice; socialism without liberty is slavery and brutality.".....</p>
<p>Bakunin played a prominent role in the Geneva Conference (September 1867), and joined the Central Committee. The founding conference was attended by 6,000 people. As Bakunin rose to speak:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>the cry passed from mouth to mouth: 'Bakunin!' Garibaldi, who was in the chair, stood up, advanced a few steps and embraced him. This solemn meeting of two old and tried warriors of the revolution produced an astonishing impression... Everyone rose and there was a prolonged and enthusiastic clapping of hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Bern" title="Bern">Bern</a> Congress of the League (1868) he and other socialists (<a href="/wiki/%C3%89lis%C3%A9e_Reclus" title="Élisée Reclus">Élisée Reclus</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aristide_Rey&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aristide Rey (page does not exist)">Aristide Rey</a>, Jaclard, <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Fanelli" title="Giuseppe Fanelli">Giuseppe Fanelli</a>, N. Joukovsky, V. Mratchkovsky and others) found themselves in a minority. They seceded from the League establishing their own <a href="/wiki/International_Alliance_of_Socialist_Democracy" title="International Alliance of Socialist Democracy">International Alliance of Socialist Democracy</a> which adopted a revolutionary socialist program.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_First_International_and_the_rise_of_the_anarchist_movement">The First International and the rise of the anarchist movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The First International and the rise of the anarchist movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bakunin_speaking.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1721" data-file-width="1263" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Bakunin_speaking.png/220px-Bakunin_speaking.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Bakunin_speaking.png/330px-Bakunin_speaking.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Bakunin_speaking.png/440px-Bakunin_speaking.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bakunin_speaking.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bakunin speaking to members of the IWA at the Basel Congress in 1869</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%91%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BD,_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%B8%D0%BB_%D0%90%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87_(1814-1876;_%D0%A6%D1%8E%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%85,_1872,_J.Ganz).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="502" data-file-width="314" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/%D0%91%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BD%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%B8%D0%BB_%D0%90%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87_%281814-1876%3B_%D0%A6%D1%8E%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%85%2C_1872%2C_J.Ganz%29.jpg/175px-%D0%91%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BD%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%B8%D0%BB_%D0%90%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87_%281814-1876%3B_%D0%A6%D1%8E%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%85%2C_1872%2C_J.Ganz%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/%D0%91%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BD%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%B8%D0%BB_%D0%90%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87_%281814-1876%3B_%D0%A6%D1%8E%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%85%2C_1872%2C_J.Ganz%29.jpg/263px-%D0%91%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BD%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%B8%D0%BB_%D0%90%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87_%281814-1876%3B_%D0%A6%D1%8E%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%85%2C_1872%2C_J.Ganz%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f9/%D0%91%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BD%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%B8%D0%BB_%D0%90%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87_%281814-1876%3B_%D0%A6%D1%8E%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%85%2C_1872%2C_J.Ganz%29.jpg 2x" width="175"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%91%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BD,_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%B8%D0%BB_%D0%90%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87_(1814-1876;_%D0%A6%D1%8E%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%85,_1872,_J.Ganz).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bakunin in 1872</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bakunin_Grab_02_11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1575" data-file-width="1049" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Bakunin_Grab_02_11.jpg/220px-Bakunin_Grab_02_11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Bakunin_Grab_02_11.jpg/330px-Bakunin_Grab_02_11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Bakunin_Grab_02_11.jpg/440px-Bakunin_Grab_02_11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bakunin_Grab_02_11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bakunin's grave at the Bremgarten cemetery in Bern</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1868, Bakunin joined the Geneva section of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_International" title="First International">First International</a>, in which he remained very active until he was expelled from the International by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and his followers at the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Congress_(1872)" title="Hague Congress (1872)">Hague Congress</a> in 1872. Bakunin was instrumental in establishing branches of the International in Italy and Spain.</p>
<p>In 1869, the <a href="/wiki/International_Alliance_of_Socialist_Democracy" title="International Alliance of Socialist Democracy">Social Democratic Alliance</a> was refused entry to the First International, on the grounds that it was an international organisation in itself, and only national organisations were permitted membership in the International. The Alliance dissolved and the various groups which it comprised joined the International separately.</p>
<p>Between 1869 and 1870, Bakunin became involved with the Russian revolutionary <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Nechayev" title="Sergey Nechayev">Sergey Nechayev</a> in a number of clandestine projects. However, Bakunin broke with Nechaev over what he described as the latter's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a>" methods, by which all means were justified to achieve revolutionary ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1870 Bakunin led a failed uprising in <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a> on the principles later exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Commune" title="Paris Commune">Paris Commune</a>, calling for a general uprising in response to the collapse of the French government during the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a>, seeking to transform an imperialist conflict into social revolution. In his <i>Letters to A Frenchman on the Present Crisis</i>, he argued for a revolutionary alliance between the working class and the peasantry, advocated a system of militias with elected officers as part of a system of self-governing communes and workplaces, and argued the time was ripe for revolutionary action:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>we must spread our principles, not with words but with deeds, for this is the most popular, the most potent, and the most irresistible form of propaganda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marxists.org_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-marxists.org-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>These ideas and corresponded strikingly closely with the program of the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Commune" title="Paris Commune">Paris Commune</a> of 1871, much of which was developed by followers of <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a>; Marxists were almost entirely absent from the Commune. Bakunin was a strong supporter of the Commune, which was brutally suppressed by the French government. He saw the Commune as above all a "rebellion against the State," and commended the Communards for rejecting not only the State but also revolutionary dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-29">[29]</a></sup> In a series of powerful pamphlets, he defended the Commune and the First International against the Italian nationalist <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Mazzini" title="Giuseppe Mazzini">Giuseppe Mazzini</a>, thereby winning over many Italian republicans to the International and the cause of revolutionary socialism.</p>
<p>Bakunin's disagreements with Marx, which led to the attempt by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marx_party" title="Marx party">Marx party</a> to expel him at the Hague Congress (see below), illustrated the growing divergence between the "<a href="/wiki/Anti-authoritarianism" title="Anti-authoritarianism">anti-authoritarian</a>" sections of the International, which advocated the direct revolutionary action and organization of the workers and peasants in order to abolish the state and capitalism, and the sections allied with Marx, which advocated the conquest of political power by the working class. Bakunin was "Marx’s flamboyant chief opponent", and "presciently warned against the emergence of a communist authoritarianism that would take power over working people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bakunin.27s_Maxim">Bakunin's Maxim</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bakunin's Maxim">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Anti-authoritarianism" title="Anti-authoritarianism">anti-authoritarian</a> majority, which included most sections of the International, created their own First International at the <a href="/wiki/Saint-Imier" title="Saint-Imier">St. Imier</a> <a href="/wiki/Anarchist_St._Imier_International" title="Anarchist St. Imier International">Congress</a>, adopted a revolutionary anarchist program, and repudiated the Hague resolutions, rescinding Bakunin's alleged expulsion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Although Bakunin accepted elements of Marx’s <a href="/wiki/Class_analysis" title="Class analysis">class analysis</a> and theories regarding capitalism, acknowledging "Marx’s genius", he thought Marx's analysis was one-sided, and that Marx's methods would compromise the social revolution. More importantly, Bakunin criticized "<a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarian socialism</a>" (which he associated with Marxism) and the concept of <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat" title="Dictatorship of the proletariat">dictatorship of the proletariat</a> which he adamantly refused. <span id="Maxim"></span></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>If you took the most ardent revolutionary, vested him in absolute power, within a year he would be worse than the Tsar himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Bakunin retired to <a href="/wiki/Lugano" title="Lugano">Lugano</a> in 1873 and died in <a href="/wiki/Bern" title="Bern">Bern</a> on 1 July 1876.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bakunin.27s_Grave">Bakunin's Grave</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bakunin's Grave">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bakunin_Bronze_by_Garbade.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3137" data-file-width="4167" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Bakunin_Bronze_by_Garbade.jpg/220px-Bakunin_Bronze_by_Garbade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Bakunin_Bronze_by_Garbade.jpg/330px-Bakunin_Bronze_by_Garbade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Bakunin_Bronze_by_Garbade.jpg/440px-Bakunin_Bronze_by_Garbade.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bakunin_Bronze_by_Garbade.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bakunin's grave 2015</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>His grave can be found in <i>Bremgarten Cemetery</i> of <a href="/wiki/Bern" title="Bern">Bern</a>, box 9021, <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Bakunin_Grab_03_11.jpg" title="c:File:Bakunin Grab 03 11.jpg">grave 68</a>. His original epitaph reads: "Remember those who sacrificed everything for the freedom of their country". In 2015 the commemorative plate was replaced in form of a bronze portrait of Bakunin by Swiss artist <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Garbade" title="Daniel Garbade">Daniel Garbade</a> containing Bakunin's quote: "By striving to do the impossible, man has always achieved what is possible". It was sponsored by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dadaist" title="Dadaist">Dadaists</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cabaret_Voltaire_(Zurich)" title="Cabaret Voltaire (Zurich)">Cabaret Voltaire</a> Zurich, who adopted Bakunin post mortem.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thought">Thought</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Thought">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bakunin's political beliefs rejected <a href="/wiki/Statism" title="Statism">statist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchical</a> systems of power in every name and shape, from the idea of God downwards, and every form of hierarchical authority, whether emanating from the will of a <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a> or even from a state that allowed <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a>. He wrote in <i>Dieu et l'État</i> (<i>God and the State</i>):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-God_and_the_State_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-God_and_the_State-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The liberty of man consists solely in this, that he obeys the laws of nature because he has himself recognized them as such, and not because they have been imposed upon him externally by any foreign will whatsoever, human or divine, collective or individual.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Bakunin similarly rejected the notion of any privileged position or class, since the social and economic inequality implied by class systems (as well as systems of national and gender oppression) were incompatible with individual freedom. Whereas <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a> insisted that <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free markets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constitutionalism" title="Constitutionalism">constitutional</a> governments enabled individual freedom, Bakunin insisted that both capitalism and the state, in any form, were incompatible with the individual freedom of the working class and peasantry.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>it is the peculiarity of privilege and of every privileged position to kill the intellect and heart of man. The privileged man, whether he be privileged politically or economically, is a man depraved in intellect and heart.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Bakunin's political beliefs were based on several interrelated concepts: (1) liberty; (2) socialism; (3) federalism; (4) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-theism" title="Anti-theism">anti-theism</a>; and (5) <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>. He also developed a critique of Marxism, predicting that if the Marxists were successful in seizing power, they would create a party dictatorship "all the more dangerous because it appears as a sham expression of the people's will."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>When the people are being beaten with a stick, they are not much happier if it is called "<a href="/wiki/The_People%27s_Stick" title="The People's Stick">the People's Stick</a>".</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Mikhail Bakunin, 1873, <a href="/wiki/Statism_and_Anarchy" title="Statism and Anarchy">Statism and Anarchy</a></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authority_and_freethought">Authority and <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">freethought</a></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Authority and freethought">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bakunin thought that "Does it follow that I reject all authority? Far from me such a thought. In the matter of boots, I refer to the authority of the bootmaker; concerning houses, canals, or railroads, I consult that of the architect or the engineer. For such or such special knowledge I apply to such or such a savant. But I allow neither the bootmaker nor the architect nor savant to impose his authority upon me. I listen to them freely and with all the respect merited by their intelligence, their character, their knowledge, reserving always my incontestable right of criticism and censure. I do not content myself with consulting a single authority in any special branch; I consult several; I compare their opinions, and choose that which seems to me the soundest. But I recognise no infallible authority, even in special questions; consequently, whatever respect I may have for the honesty and the sincerity of such or such individual, I have no absolute faith in any person."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marxists_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-marxists-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>He saw that</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"Therefore there is no fixed and constant authority, but a continual exchange of mutual, temporary, and, above all, voluntary authority and subordination. This same reason forbids me, then, to recognise a fixed, constant and universal authority, because there is no universal man, no man capable of grasping in all that wealth of detail, without which the application of science to life is impossible, all the sciences, all the branches of social life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marxists_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-marxists-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-theologism">Anti-theologism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Anti-theologism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Criticism_of_religion_sidebar" title="Template:Criticism of religion sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Criticism_of_religion_sidebar" title="Template talk:Criticism of religion sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Criticism_of_religion_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>According to political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schmitt" title="Carl Schmitt">Carl Schmitt</a>, "in comparison with later anarchists, Proudhon was a moralistic petit bourgeois who continued to subscribe to the authority of the father and the principle of the monogamous family. Bakunin was the first to give the struggle against theology the complete consistency of an absolute naturalism...For him, therefore, there was nothing negative and evil except the theological doctrine of God and sin, which stamps man as a villain in order to provide a pretext for domination and the hunger for power."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bakunin thought that religion originated from the human ability for abstract thinking and fantasizing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> According to Bakunin, religion is sustained by <a href="/wiki/Indoctrination" title="Indoctrination">indoctrination</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conformism" title="Conformism">conformism</a>. Another factor in the survival of religion is the existence of poverty, suffering and exploitation in real life, from which religion promises the salvation in the afterlife. Oppressors take advantage from religion, according to Bakunin, because many religious people reconcile themselves with injustice on earth by the promise of happiness in heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-God_and_the_State_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-God_and_the_State-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bakunin argues that oppressors receive authority from religion. Religious people are in many cases obedient to the <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priests</a>, because many religious people believe that the statements of priests are based on direct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_revelation" title="Divine revelation">divine revelation</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scripture" title="Scripture">scripture</a>. The obedience to divine revelation or scripture is considered as the ethical criteria by many religious people, because God is considered as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omniscient" title="Omniscient">omniscient</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnipotent" title="Omnipotent">omnipotent</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnibenevolent" title="Omnibenevolent">omnibenevolent</a> being. Therefore, each statement which is considered derived from an infallible God cannot be criticized by humans. According to this religious way of thinking, humans cannot know by themselves what is just, but that only God decides what is good or wrong. People who disobey the “messengers of God” are threatened with punishment in hell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-God_and_the_State_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-God_and_the_State-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Bakunin, the alternative for a religious power monopoly is the acknowledgement that all humans are equally inspired by God, but that means that multiple contradictory teachings are assigned to an infallible God, which is logically impossible. Therefore, Bakunin considers religion as necessarily authoritarian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-God_and_the_State_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-God_and_the_State-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bakunin argued in his book <i><a href="/wiki/God_and_the_State" title="God and the State">God and the State</a></i> that "the idea of God implies the abdication of human reason and justice; it is the most decisive negation of human liberty, and necessarily ends in the enslavement of mankind, in theory and practice." Consequently, Bakunin reversed <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>'s famous aphorism that if God did not exist, it would be necessary to invent Him, writing instead that "if God really existed, it would be necessary to abolish Him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-God_and_the_State_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-God_and_the_State-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bakunin was an early proponent of the term "<a href="/wiki/Political_theology" title="Political theology">political theology</a>" in his 1871 text "The Political Theology of Mazzini and the International"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> to which <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schmitt#Political_Theology" title="Carl Schmitt">Schmitt's book</a> was a response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Political theology is a branch of both <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a> and theology that investigates the ways in which theological concepts or ways of thinking underlie political, social, economic and cultural discourses.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Class_struggle_strategy_for_social_revolution">Class struggle strategy for social revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Class struggle strategy for social revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bakunin’s methods of realizing his revolutionary program were consistent with his principles. The working class and peasantry were to organize from below, through local structures interlinked on a federalist basis, "creating not only the ideas, but also the facts of the future itself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Their movements would prefigure the future, in their ideas and practices, creating the building blocks of the new society.</p>
<p>This approach was exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Syndicalism" title="Syndicalism">syndicalism</a>, an anarchist strategy championed by Bakunin, according to which trade unions would provide both the means to defend and improve workers' conditions, rights and incomes in the present, and the basis for a social revolution based upon workplace occupations. The syndicalist unions would organize the occupations, as well as provide the radically democratic structures through which workplaces would be self-managed, and the larger economy coordinated. Thus, for Bakunin, the workers' unions would "take possession of all the tools of production as well as buildings and capital."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bakunin did not reduce the revolution to syndicalist unions, however, stressing the need to organize working-class neighborhoods, as well as the unemployed. Meanwhile, the peasants were to "take the land and throw out those landlords who live by the labor of others."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marxists.org_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-marxists.org-28">[28]</a></sup> Bakunin did not dismiss the skilled workers, as is sometimes claimed; indeed, the watchmakers of the Jura region were central to the St. Imier International's creation and operations. However, at a time when unions largely ignored the unskilled, Bakunin placed great emphasis on the need to organize as well amongst "the rabble," "the great masses of the poor and exploited, the so-called "lumpenproletariat," to "inaugurate and bring to triumph the Social Revolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collectivist_anarchism">Collectivist anarchism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Collectivist anarchism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
