<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Libertarian_socialism" title="Template:Libertarian socialism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Libertarian_socialism" title="Template talk:Libertarian socialism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Libertarian_socialism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Bakunin's socialism was known as "<a href="/wiki/Collectivist_anarchism" title="Collectivist anarchism">collectivist anarchism</a>", where "socially: it seeks the confirmation of political equality by economic equality. This is not the removal of natural individual differences, but equality in the social rights of every individual from birth; in particular, equal means of subsistence, support, education, and opportunity for every child, boy or girl, until maturity, and equal resources and facilities in adulthood to create his own well-being by his own labor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Revolutionary_Catechism_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Revolutionary_Catechism-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Collectivist anarchism advocates the abolition of both the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private ownership</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a>. It instead envisions the means of production being owned collectively and controlled and managed by the producers themselves. For the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collectivization" title="Collectivization">collectivization</a> of the means of production, it was originally envisaged that workers will revolt and forcibly collectivize the means of production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patsouras.2C_Louis_2005._p._54_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patsouras.2C_Louis_2005._p._54-45">[45]</a></sup> Once collectivization takes place, money would be abolished to be replaced with <a href="/wiki/Labour_voucher" title="Labour voucher">labour notes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worker" title="Worker">workers</a>' salaries would be determined in democratic organizations based on job difficulty and the amount of time they contributed to production. These salaries would be used to purchase goods in a communal market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critique_of_Marxism">Critique of Marxism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Critique of Marxism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The dispute between Mikhail Bakunin and Karl Marx highlighted the differences between <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>. He strongly rejected Marx's concept of the "<a href="/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat" title="Dictatorship of the proletariat">dictatorship of the proletariat</a>", a concept that vanguardist socialism including Marxist–Leninism would use to justify one-party rule from above by a party 'representing' the proletariat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GW158_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-GW158-47">[47]</a></sup> Bakunin insisted that revolutions must be led by the people directly while any "enlightened elite" must only exert influence by remaining "<a href="/wiki/Invisible_dictatorship" title="Invisible dictatorship">invisible</a>...not imposed on anyone...[and] deprived of all official rights and significance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struggle.ws_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Struggle.ws-48">[48]</a></sup> He held that the state should be immediately abolished because all forms of government eventually lead to oppression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GW158_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-GW158-47">[47]</a></sup> Libertarian Marxists argue Marx used the phrase to mean the worker control at the point of production, not a party, would still be a state until society is reorganized according to socialist principles.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>They [the Marxists] maintain that only a dictatorship—their dictatorship, of course—can create the will of the people, while our answer to this is: No dictatorship can have any other aim but that of self-perpetuation, and it can beget only slavery in the people tolerating it; freedom can be created only by freedom, that is, by a universal rebellion on the part of the people and free organization of the toiling masses from the bottom up.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Mikhail Bakunin, <i>Statism and Anarchism</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>...we are convinced that liberty without socialism is privilege and injustice; and that socialism without liberty is slavery and brutality.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Mikhail Bakunin, <i>Federalism, Socialism, Anti-Theologism</i>, 1867<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>While both social anarchists and Marxists share the same final goal, the creation of a free, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egalitarian" title="Egalitarian">egalitarian</a> society <a href="/wiki/Classless_society" title="Classless society">without social classes</a> and government, they strongly disagree on how to achieve this goal. Anarchists believe that the classless, stateless society should be established by the <a href="/wiki/Direct_action" title="Direct action">direct action</a> of the masses, culminating in <a href="/wiki/Social_revolution" title="Social revolution">social revolution</a>, and refuse any intermediate stage such as the <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat" title="Dictatorship of the proletariat">dictatorship of the proletariat</a>, on the basis that such a dictatorship will become a self-perpetuating fundament. For Bakunin, the fundamental contradiction is that for the Marxists, "anarchism or freedom is the aim, while the state and dictatorship is the means, and so, in order to free the masses, they have first to be enslaved."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, Bakunin also wrote of meeting Marx in 1844 that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>As far as learning was concerned, Marx was, and still is, incomparably more advanced than I. I knew nothing at that time of political economy, I had not yet rid myself of my metaphysical observations... He called me a sentimental idealist and he was right; I called him a vain man, perfidious and crafty, and I also was right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Bakunin found Marx's economic analysis very useful and began the job of translating <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Das_Kapital" title="Das Kapital">Das Kapital</a></i> into Russian. In turn Marx wrote of the rebels in the Dresden insurrection of 1848 that "In the Russian refugee Michael Bakunin they found a capable and cool headed leader."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Marx wrote to Engels of meeting Bakunin in 1864 after his escape to Siberia saying "On the whole he is one of the few people whom I find not to have retrogressed after 16 years, but to have developed further."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bakunin has sometimes been called the first theorist of the "<a href="/wiki/New_class" title="New class">new class</a>", meaning that a 'class' of intellectuals and bureaucrats running the state in the name of the people or the proletariat – but in reality in their own interests alone. Bakunin argued that the "State has always been the patrimony of some privileged class: a priestly class, an aristocratic class, a bourgeois class. And finally, when all the other classes have exhausted themselves, the State then becomes the patrimony of the bureaucratic class and then falls—or, if you will, rises—to the position of a machine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Federalism">Federalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Federalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalism</a>, Bakunin meant the organization of society "from the base to the summit—from the circumference to the center—according to the principles of <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_association" title="Freedom of association">free association</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federation</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Revolutionary_Catechism_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Revolutionary_Catechism-44">[44]</a></sup> Consequently, society would be organized "on the basis of the absolute freedom of individuals, of the productive associations, and of the communes," with "every individual, every association, every commune, every region, every nation" having "the absolute right to self-determination, to associate or not to associate, to ally themselves with whomever they wish."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Revolutionary_Catechism_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Revolutionary_Catechism-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberty">Liberty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Liberty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By "liberty", Bakunin did not mean an abstract ideal but a concrete reality based on the equal liberty of others. In a <a href="/wiki/Positive_liberty" title="Positive liberty">positive</a> sense, liberty consists of "the fullest development of all the faculties and powers of every human being, by education, by scientific training, and by material prosperity." Such a conception of liberty is "eminently social, because it can only be realized in society," not in isolation. In a <a href="/wiki/Negative_liberty" title="Negative liberty">negative</a> sense, liberty is "the revolt of the individual against all divine, collective, and individual authority."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materialism">Materialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Materialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bakunin denied religious concepts of a supernatural sphere, and advocated a '<a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialist</a>' explanation of natural phenomena: "the manifestations of organic life, chemical properties and reactions, electricity, light, warmth and the natural attraction of physical bodies, constitute in our view so many different but no less closely interdependent variants of that totality of real beings which we call matter" (<i>Selected Writings</i>, page 219). The "mission of science is, by observation of the general relations of passing and real facts, to establish the general laws inherent in the development of the phenomena of the physical and social world."</p>
<p>However, his materialism was different to that of Marx, in that it stressed the importance of non-economic factors in human affairs, including ideas and cultures.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_revolutionary_potential_of_the_proletariat_vs_the_lumpenproletariat_and_the_peasantry">The revolutionary potential of the proletariat vs the <i>lumpenproletariat</i> and the peasantry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: The revolutionary potential of the proletariat vs the lumpenproletariat and the peasantry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bakunin had a different view as compared to Marx's on the revolutionary potential of the <i><a href="/wiki/Lumpenproletariat" title="Lumpenproletariat">lumpenproletariat</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">proletariat</a>. As such "Both agreed that the proletariat would play a key role, but for Marx the proletariat was the exclusive, leading revolutionary agent while Bakunin entertained the possibility that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peasants" title="Peasants">peasants</a> and even the lumpenproletariat (the unemployed, common criminals, etc.) could rise to the occasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Bakunin "considers workers' integration in capital as destructive of more primary revolutionary forces. For Bakunin, the revolutionary archetype is found in a peasant milieu (which is presented as having longstanding insurrectionary traditions, as well as a communist archetype in its current social form—the peasant commune) and amongst educated unemployed youth, assorted marginals from all classes, brigands, robbers, the impoverished masses, and those on the margins of society who have escaped, been excluded from, or not yet subsumed in the discipline of emerging industrial work...in short, all those whom Marx sought to include in the category of the lumpenproletariat."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bakunin is remembered as a major figure in the history of anarchism and as an opponent of Marxism, especially of Marx's idea of <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat" title="Dictatorship of the proletariat">dictatorship of the proletariat</a>, and for his predictions that Marxist regimes would be one-party dictatorships over the proletariat, not of the proletariat itself. <i><a href="/wiki/God_and_the_State" title="God and the State">God and the State</a></i> was translated multiple times by other anarchists, such as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Tucker" title="Benjamin Tucker">Benjamin Tucker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marie_Le_Compte" title="Marie Le Compte">Marie Le Compte</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Emma_Goldman" title="Emma Goldman">Emma Goldman</a>, and he continues to be an influence on modern-day anarchists, such as <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Bakunin biographer <a href="/wiki/Mark_Leier" title="Mark Leier">Mark Leier</a> has asserted that "Bakunin had a significant influence on later thinkers, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enrico_Malatesta" title="Enrico Malatesta">Enrico Malatesta</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wobblies" title="Wobblies">Wobblies</a> and Spanish anarchists in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Civil War</a> to <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Marcuse" title="Herbert Marcuse">Herbert Marcuse</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E.P._Thompson" title="E.P. Thompson">E.P. Thompson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Postman" title="Neil Postman">Neil Postman</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.S._Neill" title="A.S. Neill">A.S. Neill</a>, down to the anarchists gathered these days under the banner of 'anti-<a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sale_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sale-3">[3]</a></sup> In short, Bakunin has had a major influence on labour, peasant and leftwing movements, although this was overshadowed from the 1920s by the rise of Marxist regimes. With the collapse of those regimes – and growing awareness of how closely those regimes corresponded to the dictatorships Bakunin predicted – Bakunin's ideas have rapidly gained ground amongst activists, in some cases again overshadowing Marxism.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence.2C_revolution.2C_and_.22Invisible_dictatorship.22">Violence, revolution, and "Invisible dictatorship"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title='Edit section: Violence, revolution, and "Invisible dictatorship"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Invisible_dictatorship" title="Invisible dictatorship">Invisible dictatorship</a></div>
<p>Bakunin has been accused of being a closet authoritarian by Marxists, Leninists, and other authoritarian Leftists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In his letter to Albert Richard, he wrote that</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[t]here is only one power and one dictatorship whose organisation is salutary and feasible: it is that collective, invisible dictatorship of those who are allied in the name of our principle.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Charles A. Madison claimed that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>He [Bakunin] rejected political action as a means of abolishing the state and developed the doctrine of revolutionary conspiracy under autocratic leadership– disregarding the conflict of this principle with his philosophy of anarchism.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Madison contended that it was Bakunin's scheming for control of the First International that brought about his rivalry with Karl Marx and his expulsion from it in 1872. His approval of violence as a weapon against the agents of oppression led to nihilism in Russia and to individual acts of terrorism elsewhere– with the result that anarchism became generally synonymous with assassination and chaos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madison_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madison-60">[60]</a></sup> However, Bakunin's supporters argue that this "invisible dictatorship" is not a dictatorship in any conventional sense of the word, as Bakunin was careful to point out that its members would not exercise any official political power in the sense of a Leninist vanguard. Their influence would be ideological, and freely accepted:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>this dictatorship will be all the more salutary and effective for not being dressed up in any official power or extrinsic character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struggle.ws_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Struggle.ws-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Bakunin was also criticized by Marx<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-61">[61]</a></sup> and the delegates of the <a href="/wiki/International_Workingmen%27s_Association" title="International Workingmen's Association">International</a> specifically because his methods of organization were similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Nechayev" title="Sergey Nechayev">Sergey Nechayev</a>, with whom Bakunin was closely associated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> While Bakunin rebuked Nechayev upon discovery of his duplicity as well as his amoral politics, he did retain a streak of ruthlessness, as indicated by a 2 June 1870 letter: "Lies, cunning [and] entanglement [are] a necessary and marvelous means for demoralising and destroying the enemy, though <i>certainly not</i> a useful means of obtaining and attracting new friends."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, Bakunin began warning friends about Nechayev's behavior, and broke off all relations with Nechayev. Others note, moreover, that Bakunin never sought to take personal control of the International, that his secret organisations were not subject to his autocratic power, and that he condemned terrorism as counter-revolutionary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Robert M. Cutler goes further, pointing out that it is impossible fully to understand either Bakunin's participation in the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Peace_and_Freedom" title="League of Peace and Freedom">League of Peace and Freedom</a> or the <a href="/wiki/International_Alliance_of_Socialist_Democracy" title="International Alliance of Socialist Democracy">International Alliance of Socialist Democracy</a>, or his idea of a secret revolutionary organisation that is immanent in the people, without seeing that they derive from his interpretation of Hegel's dialectic from the 1840s. The script of Bakunin's dialectic, Cutler argues, gave the Alliance the purpose of providing the International with a real revolutionary organisation.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Marx's advocacy of participation in bourgeois politics, including parliamentary suffrage, would have been proof of [his being a "compromising Negative" in the language of the 1842 "Reaction in Germany" article]. It would have been Bakunin's duty, following the script defined by his dialectic, to bring the [International Working-Men's Association] to a recognition of its true role. [Bakunin's] desire to merge first the League and then the Alliance with the International derived from a conviction that the revolutionaries in the International should never cease to be penetrated to every extremity by the spirit of Revolution. Just as, in Bakunin's dialectic, the consistent Negatives needed the compromisers in order to vanquish them and thereby realize the Negative's true essence, so Bakunin, in the 1860s, needed the International in order to transform its activity into uncompromising Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antisemitism">Antisemitism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Antisemitism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In some of his pieces, Bakunin espouses blatantly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antisemitic" title="Antisemitic">antisemitic</a> views, and has been severely criticized for this part of his writing, not least by anarchists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Bakunin used anti-Jewish sentiments that suggested a Jewish system of global exploitation;</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>This whole Jewish world, comprising a single exploiting sect, a kind of blood sucking people, a kind of organic destructive collective parasite, going beyond not only the frontiers of states, but of political opinion, this world is now, at least for the most part, at the disposal of Marx on the one hand, and of Rothschild on the other... This may seem strange. What can there be in common between socialism and a leading bank? The point is that authoritarian socialism, Marxist communism, demands a strong centralisation of the state. And where there is centralisation of the state, there must necessarily be a central bank, and where such a bank exists, the parasitic Jewish nation, speculating with the Labour of the people, will be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>However, antisemitism was not central to Bakunin's work, which stressed <a href="/wiki/Proletarian_internationalism" title="Proletarian internationalism">proletarian internationalism</a> and a class analysis of society.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Significantly, Jews would always play a large and prominent role in the anarchist movement, among them <a href="/wiki/Emma_Goldman" title="Emma Goldman">Emma Goldman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Berkman" title="Alexander Berkman">Alexander Berkman</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voline" title="Voline">Voline</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_nationalism">Early nationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Early nationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his pre-anarchist years, Bakunin's politics were essentially a <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_nationalism" title="Left-wing nationalism">left-wing form of nationalism</a> – specifically, a focus on East Europe and Russian affairs. While Bakunin, at this time, located the national liberation and democratic struggles of the Slavs in a larger European revolutionary process, he did not pay much attention to other regions. This aspect of his thought dates from before he became an anarchist, and his anarchist works consistently envisaged a global social revolution, including both Africa and Asia. Bakunin as anarchist continued to stress the importance of national liberation, but he now insisted that this issue had to be solved as part of the social revolution. The same problem that (in his view) dogged Marxist revolutionary strategy (the capture of revolution by small elite, which would then oppress the masses) would also arise in independence struggles led by <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, unless the working class and peasantry created an anarchy:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I feel myself always the patriot of all oppressed fatherlands. . . Nationality. . . is a historic, local fact which, like all real and harmless facts, has the right to claim general acceptance. . . Every people, like every person, is involuntarily that which it is and therefore has a right to be itself. . . Nationality is not a principle; it is a legitimate fact, just as individuality is. Every nationality, great or small, has the incontestable right to be itself, to live according to its own nature. This right is simply the corollary of the general principal of freedom. -Bukunin [quoted by Alfredo M. Bonanno in Anarchism and the National Liberation Struggle, pp. 19–20]</p>
</blockquote>
<p>When Bakunin visited Japan after his escape from Siberia, he was not really involved in its politics or with the Japanese peasants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> This might be taken as evidence of a basic disinterest in Asia, but that would be incorrect. Bakunin stopped over briefly in Japan as part of a hurried flight from twelve years of imprisonment, a marked man racing across the world to his European home; he had neither Japanese contacts nor any facility in the Japanese language; the small number of expatriate newspapers by Europeans published in China and Japan provided no insights into local revolutionary conditions or possibilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Besides, Bakunin's conversion to anarchism came in 1865, towards the end of his life, and four years after his time in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works_of_Bakunin">Works of Bakunin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikhail_Bakunin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Works of Bakunin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bakunin_and_Anarchism_poster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="885" data-file-width="703" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Bakunin_and_Anarchism_poster.jpg/170px-Bakunin_and_Anarchism_poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Bakunin_and_Anarchism_poster.jpg/255px-Bakunin_and_Anarchism_poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Bakunin_and_Anarchism_poster.jpg/340px-Bakunin_and_Anarchism_poster.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/God_and_the_State" title="God and the State">God and the State</a></i>, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/048622483X">ISBN 0-486-22483-X</a></li>
<li><i>Bakunin on anarchism</i> / edited, translated and with an introduction by <a href="/wiki/Sam_Dolgoff" title="Sam Dolgoff">Sam Dolgoff</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> preface by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Avrich" title="Paul Avrich">Paul Avrich</a>.—New York : Knopf, originally published in 1971 as <i>Bakunin on anarchy</i>. Includes <a href="/wiki/James_Guillaume" title="James Guillaume">James Guillaume</a>’s <i>Bakunin—A Biographical Sketch.</i> <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0043210120">ISBN 0043210120</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Michael Bakunin: Selected Writings</i>, ed. A. Lehning. New York: Grove Press, 1974. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0802100201">ISBN 0802100201</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Statism_and_Anarchy" title="Statism and Anarchy">Statism and Anarchy</a></i>, Cambridge University Press, 1990. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0521361826">ISBN 0521361826</a>.</li>
<li><i>No Gods No Masters</i> by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Gu%C3%A9rin" title="Daniel Guérin">Daniel Guérin</a>. Edinburgh : AK Press, 1998. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1873176643">ISBN 1873176643</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Anarchism:_A_Documentary_History_of_Libertarian_Ideas" title="Anarchism: A Documentary History of Libertarian Ideas">Anarchism: A Documentary History of Libertarian Ideas</a>, Volume 1: From Anarchy to Anarchism (300CE-1939)</i>, ed. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Graham_(historian)" title="Robert Graham (historian)">Robert Graham</a>. Montreal ; New York : Black Rose Books, 2005. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1551642514">ISBN 1551642514</a>.</li>
<li><i>The Political Philosophy of Bakunin</i> edited by G. P. Maximoff, including "Mikhail Bakunin—a Biographical Sketch" by Max Nettlau</li>
<li><i>The Basic Bakunin: Writings 1869–1871</i>, ed. Robert M. Cutler. New York: Prometheus Books, 1992. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0879757450">ISBN 0879757450</a>.</li>
<li><i>Mikhail Bakunin: The Philosophical Basis of his Anarchism</i>, by Paul McLaughlin (New York: Algora Publishing, 2002, Paperback Edition <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1892941848">ISBN 1-892941-84-8</a>)</li>
<li><i>Michała Bakunina filozofia negacji</i>, by Jacek Uglik. Warsaw: Aletheia, 2007. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9788361182085">ISBN 9788361182085</a>.</li>
<li>Bakunin's ideas are examined in depth in Lucien van der Walt and Michael Schmidt's global study of anarchism and syndicalism, <a href="/wiki/Black_Flame:_The_Revolutionary_Class_Politics_of_Anarchism_and_Syndicalism_(Counter-Power_vol._1)" title="Black Flame: The Revolutionary Class Politics of Anarchism and Syndicalism (Counter-Power vol. 1)">Black Flame: The Revolutionary Class Politics of Anarchism and Syndicalism (Counter-Power vol. 1)</a>, with Bakunin described (along with his compatriot <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a>) as one of the two most important figures in anarchist history.</li>
</ul>
<p>English translations of Bakunin are generally rare when compared to the comprehensive editions in French (by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Lehning" title="Arthur Lehning">Arthur Lehning</a>), Spanish and German. An eight-volume complete works of Bakunin in English is to be published at some point in the future by <a href="/wiki/AK_Press" title="AK Press">AK Press</a>; according to <a href="/wiki/Ramsey_Kanaan" title="Ramsey Kanaan">Ramsey Kanaan</a> these will likely be published yearly for eight years in hardcover format.</p>
<p>Madelaine Grawitz’s biography (Paris: Calmann Lévy 2000) remains to be translated.</p>
<p>The standard but hostile English-language biography is by <a href="/wiki/E._H._Carr" title="E. H. Carr">E. H. Carr</a>. A new biography, <i>Bakunin: The Creative Passion</i>, by Mark Leier, was published by St. Martin’s Press August 22, 2006, hardcover, 320 pages, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0312305389">ISBN 0-312-30538-9</a></p>

<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cutler, Robert M., "Introduction" to <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Basic_Bakunin:_Writings,_1869-1871&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Basic Bakunin: Writings, 1869-1871 (page does not exist)">The Basic Bakunin: Writings, 1869-1871</a></i> (Buffalo, N.Y.: Prometheus Books, 1992), p. 27, <a class="external free" href="http://www.robertcutler.org/bakunin/basic/intro.html" rel="nofollow">http://www.robertcutler.org/bakunin/basic/intro.html</a>, retrieved 2010-12-29. Cutler also cites Bakunin's "Letter to Pablo," reproduced in Max Nettlau, <i>Michael Bakunin: Eine Biographie</i> (London: By the Author, 1896–1900), p. 284, where Bakunin maintains that the "powerful but always invisible revolutionary collectivity" leaves the "full development [of the revolution] to the revolutionary movement of the masses and the most absolute liberty to their social organization,... but always seeing to it that this movement and this organization should never be able to reconstitute any authorities, governments, or States and always combatting all ambitions collective (such as Marx's) [<i>sic</i> in the original] as well as individual, by the natural, never official, influence of every member of our [International] Alliance [of Socialist-Democracy]."</span></li>
